</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1289143401">@media(max-width:640px){body:not(.skin-minerva) .mw-parser-output .infobox{width:100%!important}body:not(.skin-minerva) .mw-parser-output .infobox th{width:50%}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:transparent}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}.mw-parser-output .infobox.biota .taxobox-edit-taxonomy img{background:transparent!important}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox{margin-top:0.5em}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox tr.taxonrow2 td{padding:2px 10px}</style>
<p><b>Mosquitoes</b>, the <b>Culicidae</b>, are a <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> of small <a href="/wiki/Diptera" class="mw-redirect" title="Diptera">flies</a> consisting of 3,600 <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. The word <i>mosquito</i> (formed by <i><a href="/wiki/Musca_(fly)" title="Musca (fly)">mosca</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Diminutive" title="Diminutive">diminutive</a> <i>-ito</i>)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is Spanish and Portuguese for <i>little fly</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-isbn0-19-861271-0&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-19-861271-0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mosquitoes have a slender, <a href="/wiki/Segmented_body" class="mw-redirect" title="Segmented body">segmented body</a>, one pair of wings, three pairs of long hair-like legs, and specialized, highly elongated, <a href="/wiki/Piercing-sucking_mouthpart" class="mw-redirect" title="Piercing-sucking mouthpart">piercing-sucking mouthparts</a>. All mosquitoes drink <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> from <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a>; females of many species have adapted to also <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">drink blood</a>. The group diversified during the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period. <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biologists" class="mw-redirect" title="Evolutionary biologists">Evolutionary biologists</a> view mosquitoes as <a href="/wiki/Micropredator" class="mw-redirect" title="Micropredator">micropredators</a>, small animals that <a href="/wiki/Parasitise" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasitise">parasitise</a> larger ones by drinking their blood without immediately killing them. Medical <a href="/wiki/Parasitologists" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasitologists">parasitologists</a> view mosquitoes as <a href="/wiki/Vectors_of_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Vectors of disease">vectors of disease</a>, carrying <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoan</a> parasites or <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> or <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viral</a> <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> from one <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> to another.
</p><p>The mosquito lifecycle consists of four stages: <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">egg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Adult" title="Adult">adult</a>. Eggs are laid on the water surface; they hatch into <a href="/wiki/Motile" class="mw-redirect" title="Motile">motile</a> larvae that feed on aquatic <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organic_material" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic material">organic material</a>. These larvae are important food sources for many freshwater animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly_nymphs" class="mw-redirect" title="Dragonfly nymphs">dragonfly nymphs</a>, many fish, and some birds. Adult females of many species have mouthparts adapted to pierce the skin of a <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">feed on blood</a> of a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> hosts, and some <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, primarily other <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>. Some species only produce eggs after a blood meal.
</p><p>The mosquito's <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a> is transferred to the host during the bite, and can cause an itchy <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rash</a>. In addition, blood-feeding species can ingest pathogens while biting and transmit them to other hosts. Those species include vectors of <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_disease" title="Parasitic disease">parasitic diseases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Filariasis" title="Filariasis">filariasis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arboviral" class="mw-redirect" title="Arboviral">arboviral</a> diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a>. By transmitting diseases, mosquitoes cause the deaths of over one million people each year.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Description_and_life_cycle">Description and life cycle</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosquito_Life-Cycle.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mosquito_Life-Cycle.svg/330px-Mosquito_Life-Cycle.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mosquito_Life-Cycle.svg/960px-Mosquito_Life-Cycle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="720" /></a><figcaption></figcaption></figure>
<p>Like all flies, mosquitoes go through four stages in their lifecycles: <a href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (biology)">egg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Imago" title="Imago">adult</a>. The first three stages—egg, larva, and pupa—are largely aquatic,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the eggs usually being laid in stagnant water.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wigglesworth&#95;1933&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wigglesworth_1933-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They hatch to become <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a>, which feed, grow, and molt until they change into <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupae</a>. The adult mosquito emerges from the mature pupa as it floats at the water surface. Mosquitoes have adult lifespans ranging from as short as a week to around a month. Some species overwinter as adults in <a href="/wiki/Diapause" title="Diapause">diapause</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Adult">Adult</h3></div>
<p>Mosquitoes have one pair of wings, with distinct scales on the surface. Their wings are long and narrow, while the legs are long and thin. The body, usually grey or black, is slender, and typically 3–6 mm long. When at rest, mosquitoes hold their first pair of legs outwards, whereas the somewhat similar <a href="/wiki/Chironomidae" title="Chironomidae">Chironomid</a> midges hold these legs forwards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">Anopheles</a></i> mosquitoes can fly for up to four hours continuously at 1 to 2&#160;km/h (0.62 to 1.24&#160;mph),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> traveling up to 12&#160;km (7.5&#160;mi) in a night. Males beat their wings between 450 and 600 times per second, <a href="/wiki/Indirect_flight" class="mw-redirect" title="Indirect flight">driven indirectly</a> by muscles which vibrate the thorax.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mosquitoes are mainly small flies; the largest are in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Toxorhynchites" title="Toxorhynchites">Toxorhynchites</a></i>, at up to 18&#160;mm (0.71&#160;in) in length and 24&#160;mm (0.94&#160;in) in wingspan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cook&#95;2009&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook_2009-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Those in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aedes" title="Aedes">Aedes</a></i> are much smaller, with a wingspan of 2.8 to 4.4&#160;mm (0.11 to 0.17&#160;in).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-UF/IFAS&#95;A.&#95;gambiae&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UF/IFAS_A._gambiae-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Mosquitoes can develop from egg to adult in hot weather in as few as five days, but it may take up to a month.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EPA&#95;life&#95;cycle&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_life_cycle-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At dawn or dusk, within days of pupating, males assemble in <a href="/wiki/Swarm" class="mw-redirect" title="Swarm">swarms</a>, mating when females fly in.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CDC&#95;Anopheles&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Anopheles-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The female mates only once in her lifetime, attracted by the pheromones emitted by the male.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In species that need blood for the eggs to develop, the female finds a host and drinks a full meal of blood. She then rests for two or three days to digest the meal and allow her eggs to develop. She is then ready to lay the eggs and repeat the cycle of feeding and laying.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CDC&#95;Anopheles&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Anopheles-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Females can live for up to three weeks in the wild, depending on temperature, humidity, their ability to obtain a blood meal, and avoiding being killed by their vertebrate hosts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CDC&#95;Anopheles&#95;14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Anopheles-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Peach-Gries-2020&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peach-Gries-2020-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="center gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 305px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 300px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Culex_pipiens_diagram_en.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Anatomy of an adult female mosquito"><img alt="Anatomy of an adult female mosquito" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Culex_pipiens_diagram_en.svg/330px-Culex_pipiens_diagram_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="316" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Culex_pipiens_diagram_en.svg/960px-Culex_pipiens_diagram_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="539" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Anatomy of an adult female mosquito</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 305px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 300px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Aedes_aegypti_E-A-Goeldi_1905.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Adult yellow fever mosquito Aedes aegypti, typical of subfamily Culicinae. Male (left) has bushy antennae and longer <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts" title="Insect mouthparts">palps</a> than female (right)</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Eggs">Eggs</h3></div>
<p>The eggs of most mosquitoes are laid in stagnant water, which may be a pond, a marsh, a temporary puddle, a water-filled hole in a tree, or the water-trapping leaf axils of a <a href="/wiki/Bromeliaceae" title="Bromeliaceae">bromeliad</a>. Some lay near the water's edge while others attach their eggs to aquatic plants. A few, like <i><a href="/wiki/Opifex_fuscus" title="Opifex fuscus">Opifex fuscus</a></i>, can breed in salt-marshes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wigglesworth&#95;1933&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wigglesworth_1933-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Wyeomyia_smithii" title="Wyeomyia smithii">Wyeomyia smithii</a></i> breeds in the pitchers of <a href="/wiki/Pitcher_plant" title="Pitcher plant">pitcher plants</a>, its larvae feeding on decaying insects that have drowned there.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Oviposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Oviposition">Oviposition</a>, egg-laying, varies between species. <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">Anopheles</a></i> females fly over the water, touching down or <a href="/wiki/Dapping" title="Dapping">dapping</a> to place eggs on the surface one at a time; their eggs are roughly cigar-shaped and have floats down their sides. A female can lay 100–200 eggs in her lifetime.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CDC&#95;Anopheles&#95;14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Anopheles-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Aedes</i> females drop their eggs singly, on damp mud or other surfaces near water; their eggs hatch only when they are flooded.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Huang&#95;Walker&#95;Vulule&#95;Miller&#95;2006&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huang_Walker_Vulule_Miller_2006-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Females in genera such as <i><a href="/wiki/Culex" title="Culex">Culex</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Culiseta" title="Culiseta">Culiseta</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Uranotaenia" title="Uranotaenia">Uranotaenia</a></i> lay their eggs in floating rafts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Spielman,&#95;D&#39;Antonio&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spielman,_D&#39;Antonio-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Mansonia_(fly)" title="Mansonia (fly)">Mansonia</a></i> females in contrast lay their eggs in arrays, attached usually to the under-surfaces of waterlily pads.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Amorim&#95;Sa&#95;Rojas&#95;Santos&#95;Neto&#95;2022&#95;pp.&#95;631–637&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amorim_Sa_Rojas_Santos_Neto_2022_pp._631–637-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Clutches of eggs of most mosquito species hatch simultaneously, but <i>Aedes</i> eggs in diapause hatch irregularly over an extended period.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Huang&#95;Walker&#95;Vulule&#95;Miller&#95;2006&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huang_Walker_Vulule_Miller_2006-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 103.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 101.33333333333px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Anopheles_egg_2_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Anopheles eggs with side floats"><img alt="Anopheles eggs with side floats" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Anopheles_egg_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Anopheles_egg_2_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="102" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Anopheles_egg_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Anopheles_egg_2_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="353" data-file-height="417" /></a></span></div>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 182.66666666667px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosquito_egg_SEM.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Electron micrograph of a culicine egg"><img alt="Electron micrograph of a culicine egg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Mosquito_egg_SEM.jpg/330px-Mosquito_egg_SEM.jpg" decoding="async" width="183" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Mosquito_egg_SEM.jpg/960px-Mosquito_egg_SEM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2340" data-file-height="1536" /></a></span></div>

		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Larva">Larva</h3></div>
<p>The mosquito larva's head has prominent mouth brushes used for feeding, a large <a href="/wiki/Thorax_(insect_anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thorax (insect anatomy)">thorax</a> with no legs, and a segmented <a href="/wiki/Abdomen#Arthropoda" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>. It breathes air through a siphon on its abdomen, so must come to the surface frequently. It spends most of its time feeding on <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, bacteria, and other microbes in the water's surface layer. It dives below the surface when disturbed. It swims either by propelling itself with its mouth brushes, or by jerkily wriggling its body. It develops through several stages, or <a href="/wiki/Instar" title="Instar">instars</a>, molting each time, after which it <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphoses</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupa</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EPA&#95;life&#95;cycle&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_life_cycle-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Aedes</i> larvae, except when very young, can withstand drying; they go into <a href="/wiki/Diapause" title="Diapause">diapause</a> for several months if their pond dries out.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Huang&#95;Walker&#95;Vulule&#95;Miller&#95;2006&#95;19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huang_Walker_Vulule_Miller_2006-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="center gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:AnophelesLarvaPhoto_CDCHarryWeinburgh_publicdomain.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Anopheles larva"><img alt="Anopheles larva" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/AnophelesLarvaPhoto_CDCHarryWeinburgh_publicdomain.jpg/250px-AnophelesLarvaPhoto_CDCHarryWeinburgh_publicdomain.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="142" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/AnophelesLarvaPhoto_CDCHarryWeinburgh_publicdomain.jpg/500px-AnophelesLarvaPhoto_CDCHarryWeinburgh_publicdomain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3555" data-file-height="2528" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"><i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">Anopheles</a></i> larva</div>
		</li>

		</li>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Culex_sp_larvae.png" class="mw-file-description" title="Culex larvae plus one pupa"><img alt="Culex larvae plus one pupa" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Culex_sp_larvae.png/250px-Culex_sp_larvae.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="122" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Culex_sp_larvae.png/500px-Culex_sp_larvae.png 2x" data-file-width="1370" data-file-height="834" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"><i>Culex</i> larvae plus one pupa</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pupa">Pupa</h3></div>
<p>The head and thorax of the <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupa</a> are merged into a <a href="/wiki/Cephalothorax" title="Cephalothorax">cephalothorax</a>, with the abdomen curving around beneath it. The pupa or "tumbler" can swim actively by flipping its abdomen. Like the larva, the pupa of most species must come to the surface frequently to breathe, which they do through a pair of respiratory trumpets on their cephalothoraxes. They do not feed; they pass much of their time hanging from the surface of the water by their respiratory trumpets. If alarmed, they swim downwards by flipping their abdomens in much the same way as the larvae. If undisturbed, they soon float up again. The adult emerges from the pupa at the surface of the water and flies off.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EPA&#95;life&#95;cycle&#95;13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_life_cycle-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="center gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 405px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 400px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Underwater_view_of_mosquito_pupae_in_standing_water.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Mosquito pupae, shortly before the adults emerged. The head and thorax are fused into the cephalothorax."><img alt="Mosquito pupae, shortly before the adults emerged. The head and thorax are fused into the cephalothorax." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Underwater_view_of_mosquito_pupae_in_standing_water.jpg/500px-Underwater_view_of_mosquito_pupae_in_standing_water.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="155" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Underwater_view_of_mosquito_pupae_in_standing_water.jpg/960px-Underwater_view_of_mosquito_pupae_in_standing_water.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3765" data-file-height="1455" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Mosquito pupae, shortly before the adults emerged. The head and thorax are fused into the cephalothorax.</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Feeding_by_adults">Feeding by adults</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Diet">Diet</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anautogeny" title="Anautogeny">Anautogeny</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosquito_Tasmania_crop.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mosquito_Tasmania_crop.jpg/250px-Mosquito_Tasmania_crop.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mosquito_Tasmania_crop.jpg/500px-Mosquito_Tasmania_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1719" data-file-height="1296" /></a><figcaption>Female <i><a href="/wiki/Aedes" title="Aedes">Aedes</a></i> sp. feeding on blood from a human arm. </figcaption></figure>
<p>Both male and female mosquitoes feed on <a href="/wiki/Nectar_source" title="Nectar source">nectar</a>, aphid honeydew, and plant juices,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Peach-Gries-2020&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peach-Gries-2020-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but in many species the females are also <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">hematophagous</a> (blood-sucking) <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">ectoparasites</a>. In some of those species, a blood meal is essential for egg production;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid30846726&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30846726-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in others, it just enables the female to lay more eggs. Both plant materials and blood are useful sources of energy in the form of sugars. Blood supplies more concentrated nutrients, such as <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, but the main function of blood meals is to obtain proteins for egg production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tyagi&#95;2004&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyagi_2004-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-AMCA&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCA-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Disease vector mosquitoes like <i>Anopheles</i> and <i>Aedes</i> are <a href="/wiki/Anautogenous" class="mw-redirect" title="Anautogenous">anautogenous</a>, requiring blood to lay eggs. Some <i><a href="/wiki/Culex" title="Culex">Culex</a></i> species are partially autogenous, needing blood only for their second and subsequent clutches of eggs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The three genera of <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaya_(fly)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Malaya (fly) (page does not exist)">Malaya</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Topomyia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Topomyia (page does not exist)">Topomyia</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Toxorhynchites" title="Toxorhynchites">Toxorhynchites</a></i>, together comprising a small percentage of mosquitoes species, reproduce autogenously, never taking blood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Host_animals">Host animals</h3></div>
<p>Blood-sucking mosquitoes favour particular host species, though they are less selective when food is short. Different mosquito species favour <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> including <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. For example, <i><a href="/wiki/Culiseta_melanura" title="Culiseta melanura">Culiseta melanura</a></i> sucks the blood of <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a> birds, but as mosquito numbers rise they attack mammals including horses and humans, causing epidemics of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_equine_encephalitis_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern equine encephalitis virus">Eastern equine encephalitis virus</a> in North America.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lehane2005&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lehane2005-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Loss of blood from many bites can add up to a large volume, occasionally causing the death of <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> as large as <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-LSUAgCenter&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSUAgCenter-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">Malaria</a>-transmitting mosquitoes seek out <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a> and feed on their haemolymph,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-George&#95;Blanford&#95;Thomas&#95;Baker&#95;2014&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George_Blanford_Thomas_Baker_2014-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> impeding their development.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 161.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 159.33333333333px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Chironius_scurrulus_(Yasuni)_(cropped)_with_mosquitoes.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Feeding on a snake"><img alt="Feeding on a snake" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Chironius_scurrulus_%28Yasuni%29_%28cropped%29_with_mosquitoes.jpg/250px-Chironius_scurrulus_%28Yasuni%29_%28cropped%29_with_mosquitoes.jpg" decoding="async" width="160" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Chironius_scurrulus_%28Yasuni%29_%28cropped%29_with_mosquitoes.jpg/500px-Chironius_scurrulus_%28Yasuni%29_%28cropped%29_with_mosquitoes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="543" /></a></span></div>

		</li>

		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Finding_hosts">Finding hosts</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:How_to_attract_mosquitoes.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/How_to_attract_mosquitoes.svg/500px-How_to_attract_mosquitoes.svg.png" decoding="async" width="380" height="285" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/How_to_attract_mosquitoes.svg/960px-How_to_attract_mosquitoes.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a><figcaption>Blood-feeding female mosquitoes find their hosts using multiple cues, including exhaled <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, heat, and many different <a href="/wiki/Odorant" class="mw-redirect" title="Odorant">odorants</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Most mosquito species are <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" class="mw-redirect" title="Crepuscular">crepuscular</a>, feeding at dawn or dusk, and resting in a cool place through the heat of the day.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Asian_tiger_mosquito" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian tiger mosquito">Asian tiger mosquito</a>, are known to fly and feed during daytime.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Female mosquitoes hunt for hosts by smelling substances such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/1-Octen-3-ol" title="1-Octen-3-ol">1-octen-3-ol</a> (mushroom alcohol, found in exhaled breath) produced from the host, and through visual recognition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Semiochemical" title="Semiochemical">semiochemical</a> that most strongly attracts <i><a href="/wiki/Culex_quinquefasciatus" title="Culex quinquefasciatus">Culex quinquefasciatus</a></i> is <a href="/wiki/Nonanal" title="Nonanal">nonanal</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another attractant is <a href="/wiki/Sulcatone" title="Sulcatone">sulcatone</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A large part of the mosquito's sense of smell, or olfactory system, is devoted to sniffing out blood sources. Of 72 types of odor receptors on its antennae, at least 27 are tuned to detect chemicals found in perspiration.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <i>Aedes</i>, the search for a host takes place in two phases. First, the mosquito flies about until it detects a host's odorants; then it flies towards them, using the concentration of odorants as its guide.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Estrada&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Estrada-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mosquitoes prefer to feed on people with <a href="/wiki/Type_O_blood" class="mw-redirect" title="Type O blood">type O blood</a>, an abundance of skin bacteria, and high body heat; they also favor pregnant women.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chappell,&#95;NPR&#95;2013.07.12:&#95;Mosquitos&#39;&#95;perceptions&#95;of&#95;humans&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chappell,_NPR_2013.07.12:_Mosquitos&#39;_perceptions_of_humans-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Individuals' attractiveness to mosquitoes has a <a href="/wiki/Heritable" class="mw-redirect" title="Heritable">heritable</a>, genetically controlled component.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The multitude of characteristics in a host observed by the mosquito allows it to select a host to feed on. It activates odour and visual search behaviours that it otherwise would not use, when in presence of CO<sub>2</sub>. In terms of a mosquito's olfactory system, chemical analysis has revealed that people who are highly attractive to mosquitoes produce significantly more <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acids</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A human's unique body odour indicates that the target is actually a human host rather than some other living warm-blooded animal (as the presence of CO<sub>2</sub> shows). Body odour, composed of <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a> emitted from the skin of humans, is the most important cue used by mosquitoes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many of these volatile odor compounds (VOCs) are produced when skin-associated bacteria metabolize components of sweat and <a href="/wiki/Sebum" class="mw-redirect" title="Sebum">sebum</a>, contributing to individual variation in human odour profiles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Acree&#95;Turner&#95;1968&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acree_Turner_1968-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Variation in skin odour is caused by body weight, hormones, genetic factors, and metabolic or genetic disorders. Infections such as malaria can influence an individual's body odour. People infected by malaria produce relatively large amounts of <i>Plasmodium</i>-induced aldehydes in the skin, creating large cues for mosquitoes as it increases the attractiveness of an odour blend, imitating a "healthy" human odour. Infected individuals produce larger amounts of aldehydes <a href="/wiki/Heptanal" title="Heptanal">heptanal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Octanal" title="Octanal">octanal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nonanal" title="Nonanal">nonanal</a>. These compounds are detected by mosquito antennae. Thus, people infected with malaria are more prone to mosquito biting.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Contributing to a mosquito's ability to activate search behaviours, a mosquito's visual search system includes sensitivity to wavelengths from different colours. Mosquitoes are attracted to longer wavelengths, correlated to the colours of red and orange as seen by humans, and range through the spectrum of human skin tones. In addition, they have a strong attraction to dark, high-contrast objects, because of how longer wavelengths are perceived against a lighter-coloured background.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Culex_mosquito_proboscis.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Culex_mosquito_proboscis.jpg/250px-Culex_mosquito_proboscis.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="211" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Culex_mosquito_proboscis.jpg/500px-Culex_mosquito_proboscis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2548" data-file-height="2147" /></a><figcaption>Scanning electron microscope image of the labium tip of a <i>Culex</i> mosquito</figcaption></figure>
<p>Different species of mosquitoes have evolved different methods of identifying target hosts. Study of a domestic form and an animal-biting form of the mosquito <i>Aedes aegypti</i> showed that the evolution of preference for human odour is linked to increases in the expression of the <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_receptor" title="Olfactory receptor">olfactory receptor</a> AaegOr4. This recognises a compound present at high levels in human odour called <a href="/wiki/Sulcatone" title="Sulcatone">sulcatone</a>. However, the malaria mosquito <i>Anopheles gambiae</i> also has OR4 genes strongly activated by sulcatone, yet none of them are closely related to AaegOr4, suggesting that the two species have evolved to specialise in biting humans independently.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mouthparts">Mouthparts</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts" title="Insect mouthparts">Insect mouthparts</a></div>
<p>Female mosquito mouthparts are highly adapted to piercing skin and sucking blood. Males only drink sugary fluids, and have less specialized mouthparts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wahid&#95;Sunahara&#95;Mogi&#95;2003&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wahid_Sunahara_Mogi_2003-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Externally, the most obvious feeding structure of the mosquito is the proboscis, composed of the <a href="/wiki/Labium_(insect)" class="mw-redirect" title="Labium (insect)">labium</a>, U-shaped in section like a <a href="/wiki/Rain_gutter" title="Rain gutter">rain gutter</a>, which sheaths a bundle (fascicle) of six piercing mouthparts or stylets. These are two <a href="/wiki/Mandible_(insect_mouthpart)" title="Mandible (insect mouthpart)">mandibles</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Galea_(insects)" class="mw-redirect" title="Galea (insects)">maxillae</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts#Hypopharynx" title="Insect mouthparts">hypopharynx</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Labrum_(arthropod_mouthpart)" title="Labrum (arthropod mouthpart)">labrum</a>. The labium bends back into a bow when the mosquito begins to bite, staying in contact with the skin and guiding the stylets downwards. The extremely sharp tips of the labrum and maxillae are moved backwards and forwards to saw their way into the skin, with just one thousandth of the force that would be needed to penetrate the skin with a needle, resulting in a painless insertion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NPR&#95;mouthparts&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR_mouthparts-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Choo&#95;Buss&#95;Tan&#95;Leal&#95;2015&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Choo_Buss_Tan_Leal_2015-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zahran&#95;Sawires&#95;Hamza&#95;2022&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zahran_Sawires_Hamza_2022-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="center gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 355px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 350px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_mosquito_mouthparts.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Evolution of mosquito mouthparts, with grasshopper mouthparts (shown both in situ and separately) representing a more primitive condition. All the mouthparts except the labium are stylets, formed into a fascicle or bundle."><img alt="Evolution of mosquito mouthparts, with grasshopper mouthparts (shown both in situ and separately) representing a more primitive condition. 			<div class="gallerytext">Evolution of mosquito mouthparts, with <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshopper</a> mouthparts (shown both <i><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">in situ</a></i> and separately) representing a more primitive condition. All the mouthparts except the labium are stylets, formed into a fascicle or bundle.</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 355px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 350px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Feeding_mosquito,_mouthparts_labelled.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Mouthparts of a female mosquito while feeding on blood, showing the flexible labium sheath supporting the piercing and sucking tube which penetrates the host&#39;s skin"><img alt="Mouthparts of a female mosquito while feeding on blood, showing the flexible labium sheath supporting the piercing and sucking tube which penetrates the host&#39;s skin" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Feeding_mosquito%2C_mouthparts_labelled.svg/500px-Feeding_mosquito%2C_mouthparts_labelled.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="263" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Feeding_mosquito%2C_mouthparts_labelled.svg/960px-Feeding_mosquito%2C_mouthparts_labelled.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Mouthparts of a female mosquito while feeding on blood, showing the flexible <a href="/wiki/Labium_(insect)" class="mw-redirect" title="Labium (insect)">labium</a> sheath supporting the piercing and sucking tube which penetrates the host's skin</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Saliva">Saliva</h3></div>
<p>Mosquito saliva contains <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that aid in sugar feeding,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_agents" class="mw-redirect" title="Antimicrobial agents">antimicrobial agents</a> that control bacterial growth in the sugar meal.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For a mosquito to obtain a blood meal, it must circumvent its <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> host's physiological responses.  Mosquito saliva blocks the host's <a href="/wiki/Hemostasis" title="Hemostasis">hemostasis</a> system, with proteins that reduce <a href="/wiki/Vascular_constriction" class="mw-redirect" title="Vascular constriction">vascular constriction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood_clotting" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood clotting">blood clotting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelet</a> aggregation, to ensure the blood keeps flowing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Valenzuela&#95;Pham&#95;Garfield&#95;2002&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valenzuela_Pham_Garfield_2002-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It modulates the host's <a href="/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune response</a> via a mixture of proteins which lower <a href="/wiki/Angiogenesis" title="Angiogenesis">angiogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>, create <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Valenzuela&#95;Pham&#95;Garfield&#95;2002&#95;53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valenzuela_Pham_Garfield_2002-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-autogenerated4&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> suppress <a href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor" title="Tumor necrosis factor">tumor necrosis factor</a> release from activated <a href="/wiki/Mast_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Mast cells">mast cells</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> suppress <a href="/wiki/Interleukin" title="Interleukin">interleukin</a> (IL)-2 and <a href="/wiki/IFN-%CE%B3" class="mw-redirect" title="IFN-γ">IFN-γ</a> production,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cross&#95;Cupp&#95;Enriquez&#95;1994&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cross_Cupp_Enriquez_1994-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zeidner&#95;Higgs&#95;Happ&#95;1999&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zeidner_Higgs_Happ_1999-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> suppress <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cell</a> populations,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> decrease expression of <a href="/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferon</a>−α/β making virus infections more severe,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> increase <a href="/wiki/Natural_killer_T_cell" title="Natural killer T cell">natural killer T cells</a> in the blood, and decrease cytokine production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Egg_development_and_blood_digestion">Egg development and blood digestion</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg/250px-Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg/500px-Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2008" /></a><figcaption>An <i>Anopheles stephensi</i> female is engorged with blood and beginning to pass unwanted liquid fractions to make room in its gut for more of the solid nutrients.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Females of many blood-feeding species need a blood meal to begin the process of egg development. A sufficiently large blood meal triggers a hormonal cascade that leads to egg development.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Upon completion of feeding, the mosquito withdraws her <a href="/wiki/Proboscis" title="Proboscis">proboscis</a>, and as the gut fills up, the stomach lining secretes a <a href="/wiki/Peritrophic_matrix" title="Peritrophic matrix">peritrophic membrane</a> that surrounds the blood. This keeps the blood separate from anything else in the stomach. Like many <a href="/wiki/Hemiptera" title="Hemiptera">Hemiptera</a> that survive on dilute liquid diets, many adult mosquitoes excrete surplus liquid even when feeding. This permits females to accumulate a full meal of nutrient solids. The blood meal is digested over a period of several days.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Curic&#95;Hercog&#95;Vrselja&#95;Wagner&#95;2014&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curic_Hercog_Vrselja_Wagner_2014-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Once blood is in the stomach, the midgut synthesizes <a href="/wiki/Protease" title="Protease">protease</a> enzymes, primarily <a href="/wiki/Trypsin" title="Trypsin">trypsin</a> assisted by <a href="/wiki/Aminopeptidase" title="Aminopeptidase">aminopeptidase</a>, that hydrolyze the blood <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> into free <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>. These are used in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Vitellogenin" title="Vitellogenin">vitellogenin</a>, which in turn is made into egg yolk protein.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Billingsley&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billingsley-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Distribution">Distribution</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cosmopolitan">Cosmopolitan</h3></div>
<p>Mosquitoes have a <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitan_distribution" title="Cosmopolitan distribution">cosmopolitan distribution</a>, occurring in every land region except Antarctica and a few islands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Visindavefur.hi.is&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Visindavefur.hi.is-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Polar_climate" title="Polar climate">polar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">subpolar climate</a> of these regions is unpredictable, freezing but often warming suddenly in mid-winter, making mosquitoes emerge from pupae in diapause, and then freezing again before they can complete their life cycle.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Eggs of <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate zone</a> mosquitoes are more tolerant of cold than the eggs of species indigenous to warmer regions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many can tolerate subzero temperatures, while adults of some species can survive winter by sheltering in microhabitats such as buildings or hollow trees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In warm and humid tropical regions, some mosquito species are active for the entire year, but in temperate and cold regions they hibernate or enter <a href="/wiki/Diapause" title="Diapause">diapause</a>. <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> or subarctic mosquitoes, like some other arctic midges in families such as <a href="/wiki/Simuliidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Simuliidae">Simuliidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ceratopogonidae" title="Ceratopogonidae">Ceratopogonidae</a> may be active for only a few weeks annually as melt-water pools form on the permafrost. During that time, though, they emerge in huge numbers in some regions; a swarm may take up to 300 ml of blood per day from each animal in a <a href="/wiki/Caribou" class="mw-redirect" title="Caribou">caribou</a> herd.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-n1&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-n1-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effect_of_climate_change">Effect of climate change</h3></div>
<p>For a mosquito to transmit disease, there must be favorable seasonal conditions,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> primarily humidity, temperature, and precipitation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bai&#95;Morton&#95;Liu&#95;2013&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bai_Morton_Liu_2013-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" class="mw-redirect" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> affects the location and number of outbreaks in East Africa, Latin America, Southeast Asia and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> impacts the seasonal factors and in turn the dispersal of mosquitoes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid30120891&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30120891-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Climate models can use historic data to recreate past outbreaks and to predict the risk of vector-borne disease, based on an area's forecasted climate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid29229233&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29229233-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Mosquito-borne diseases have long been most prevalent in East Africa, Latin America, Southeast Asia, and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. An emergence in Europe was observed early in the 21st century. It is predicted that by 2030, the climate of southern Great Britain will be suitable for transmission of <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_vivax" title="Plasmodium vivax">Plasmodium vivax</a></i> malaria by <i>Anopheles</i> mosquitoes for two months of the year, and that by 2080, the same will be true for southern Scotland.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid29219091&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29219091-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">Dengue fever</a>, too, is spreading northwards with climate change. The vector, the Asian tiger mosquito <i><a href="/wiki/Aedes_albopictus" title="Aedes albopictus">Aedes albopictus</a></i>, has by 2023 established across southern Europe and as far north as much of northern France, Belgium, Holland, and both <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> and West London in England.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Horton&#95;2024&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horton_2024-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
In 2025, some specimens of <i><a href="/wiki/Culiseta_annulata" title="Culiseta annulata">Culiseta annulata</a></i> were found in <a href="/wiki/Kj%C3%B3sarhreppur" title="Kjósarhreppur">Kjósarhreppur</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, a country that had been free of mosquitoes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Ecology">Ecology</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Predators_and_parasites">Predators and parasites</h3></div>
<p>Mosquito larvae are among the commonest animals in ponds, and they form an important food source for freshwater <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>. Among the many aquatic insects that catch mosquito larvae are <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonfly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damselfly" title="Damselfly">damselfly</a> nymphs, <a href="/wiki/Whirligig_beetle" title="Whirligig beetle">whirligig beetles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_strider" class="mw-redirect" title="Water strider">water striders</a>. Vertebrate predators include fish such as catfish and the <a href="/wiki/Mosquitofish" title="Mosquitofish">mosquitofish</a>, amphibians including the <a href="/wiki/Scaphiopodidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Scaphiopodidae">spadefoot toad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Giant_tree_frog" class="mw-redirect" title="Giant tree frog">giant tree frog</a>, freshwater turtles such as the <a href="/wiki/Red-eared_slider" title="Red-eared slider">red-eared slider</a>, and birds such as ducks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Emerging adults are consumed at the pond surface by predatory flies including <a href="/wiki/Empididae" title="Empididae">Empididae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dolichopodidae" title="Dolichopodidae">Dolichopodidae</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a>. Flying adults are captured by dragonflies and damselflies, by birds such as <a href="/wiki/Apus_(bird)" title="Apus (bird)">swifts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallows</a>, and by mammals including <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Medlock&#95;Snow&#95;2008&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medlock_Snow_2008-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Mosquitoes are parasitised by <a href="/wiki/Hydrachnidia" title="Hydrachnidia">hydrachnid</a> mites, <a href="/wiki/Ciliate" title="Ciliate">ciliates</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Glaucoma_(ciliate)" title="Glaucoma (ciliate)">Glaucoma</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Microsporidia" title="Microsporidia">microsporidians</a> such as <i>Thelania</i>, and fungi including species of <a href="/wiki/Saprolegniaceae" title="Saprolegniaceae">Saprolegniaceae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entomophthoraceae" title="Entomophthoraceae">Entomophthoraceae</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Medlock&#95;Snow&#95;2008&#95;83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medlock_Snow_2008-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pollination">Pollination</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosquito_on_marigold_flower.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mosquito_on_marigold_flower.JPG/250px-Mosquito_on_marigold_flower.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="179" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mosquito_on_marigold_flower.JPG/500px-Mosquito_on_marigold_flower.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2320" data-file-height="1659" /></a><figcaption>A mosquito visiting a <a href="/wiki/Calendula" title="Calendula">marigold</a> flower for <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> </figcaption></figure>
<p>Several flowers including members of the <a href="/wiki/Asteraceae" title="Asteraceae">Asteraceae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosaceae" title="Rosaceae">Rosaceae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orchidaceae" class="mw-redirect" title="Orchidaceae">Orchidaceae</a> are <a href="/wiki/Pollinated" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollinated">pollinated</a> by mosquitoes, which visit to obtain sugar-rich <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a>. A few plant associations are specialized for mosquito pollination, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Platanthera" title="Platanthera">Platanthera</a></i> orchids which are pollinated by <i>Aedes</i> species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are attracted to flowers by a range of semiochemicals such as alcohols, aldehydes, ketones, and terpenes. They may also use visual cues including UV reflectivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mosquitoes have visited and pollinated flowers since the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period. It is possible that plant-sucking <a href="/wiki/Exaptation" title="Exaptation">exapted</a> mosquitoes to blood-sucking.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Peach-Gries-2020&#95;17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peach-Gries-2020-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Parasitism">Parasitism</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">Parasitism</a></div>
<p>Ecologically, blood-feeding mosquitoes are <a href="/wiki/Micropredator" class="mw-redirect" title="Micropredator">micropredators</a>, small animals that feed on larger animals without immediately killing them. Evolutionary biologists see this as a form of <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasitism</a>; in <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">Edward O. Wilson</a>'s phrase "Parasites ... are predators that eat prey in units of less than one."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wilson&#95;2014&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson_2014-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Micropredation is one of six major <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_ecology#Evolutionarily_stable_strategy" title="Behavioral ecology">evolutionarily stable strategies</a> within parasitism. It is distinguished by leaving the host still able to reproduce, unlike the activity of <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_castration" title="Parasitic castration">parasitic castrators</a> or <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid" title="Parasitoid">parasitoids</a>; and having multiple hosts, unlike conventional parasites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Poulin&#95;Randhawa&#95;2011&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poulin_Randhawa_2011-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Poulin&#95;Randhawa&#95;2015&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poulin_Randhawa_2015-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> From this perspective, mosquitoes are <a href="/wiki/Ectoparasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ectoparasite">ectoparasites</a>, feeding on blood from the outside of their hosts, using their piercing mouthparts, rather than entering their bodies. Unlike some other ectoparasites such as <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">fleas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louse" title="Louse">lice</a>, mosquitoes do not remain constantly on the body of the host, but visit only to feed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Poulin&#95;Randhawa&#95;2015&#95;88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poulin_Randhawa_2015-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fossil_record">Fossil record</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Culex_malariager_in_Dominican_amber.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Fossilized mosquito encased in amber" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Culex_malariager_in_Dominican_amber.jpg/250px-Culex_malariager_in_Dominican_amber.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Culex_malariager_in_Dominican_amber.jpg/500px-Culex_malariager_in_Dominican_amber.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1350" data-file-height="1800" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Culex_malariager" title="Culex malariager">Culex malariager</a></i> mosquito infected with the <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malarial</a> parasite <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_dominicana" title="Plasmodium dominicana">Plasmodium dominicana</a></i>, in <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican</a> amber of <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> age, 15–20 million years ago<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Poinar&#95;2014&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poinar_2014-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>A 2023 study suggested that <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Libanoculex&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Libanoculex (page does not exist)">Libanoculex intermedius</a></i> found in <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_amber" title="Lebanese amber">Lebanese amber</a>, dating to the <a href="/wiki/Barremian" title="Barremian">Barremian</a> age of the Early Cretaceous, around 125 million years ago was the oldest known mosquito.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However its identification as a mosquito is disputed, with other authors considering it to be a <a href="/wiki/Chaoboridae" title="Chaoboridae">chaoborid</a> fly instead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 7 other, non-contentious species of <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> mosquito are known. <i><a href="/wiki/Burmaculex" title="Burmaculex">Burmaculex</a>,</i> with 5 recognised species, (including one formerly assigned to the separate genus <i>Priscoculex</i>) known from adults, and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cretosabethes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cretosabethes (page does not exist)">Cretosabethes primaevus</a></i> known from a larva, have been found in  <a href="/wiki/Burmese_amber" title="Burmese amber">Burmese amber</a> from Myanmar, which dates to the earliest part of the <a href="/wiki/Cenomanian" title="Cenomanian">Cenomanian</a> age of the Late Cretaceous, around 99 million years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleoculicis_minutus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Paleoculicis minutus (page does not exist)">Paleoculicis minutus</a></i>, is known from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_amber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Canadian amber (page does not exist)">Canadian amber</a> from Alberta, Canada, which dates to the <a href="/wiki/Campanian" title="Campanian">Campanian</a> age of the Late Cretaceous, around 79 million years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Burmaculex</i> and <i>Paleoculicis</i> have been interpreted as a member of the extinct subfamily Burmaculicinae that split off before the common ancestors of the two major modern subfamilies Anophelinae and Culicinae diverged,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while <i>Cretosabethes</i> has been interpreted as a member of the modern Culicinae tribe <a href="/wiki/Sabethini" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabethini">Sabethini</a>, implying that the diversification of mosquitoes had already considerably progressed prior to the mid-Cretaceous, despite the sparse fossil record of the group.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been suggested based on molecular clock dating that mosquitoes originated in the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a>, but that major diversification did not take place until the Cretaceous.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_mosquito_genera" title="List of mosquito genera">List of mosquito genera</a></div>
<p>There are over 3,700<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> species of mosquitoes; 112 <a href="/wiki/Genera" class="mw-redirect" title="Genera">genera</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">described</a>. They are traditionally divided into two subfamilies, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_mosquito_genera#Subfamily_Anophelinae" title="List of mosquito genera">Anophelinae</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Culicinae" title="Culicinae">Culicinae</a>, which carry different diseases. Roughly speaking, protozoal diseases like malaria are transmitted by anophelines, while viral diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a> are transmitted by culicines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Molina-Cruz&#95;Lehmann&#95;Knöckel&#95;2013&#95;pp.&#95;530–537&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Molina-Cruz_Lehmann_Knöckel_2013_pp._530–537-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The name Culicidae was introduced by the German entomologist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wilhelm_Meigen" title="Johann Wilhelm Meigen">Johann Wilhelm Meigen</a> in his seven-volume classification published in 1818–1838.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Meigen&#95;1818&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meigen_1818-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mosquito taxonomy was advanced in 1901 when the English entomologist <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Vincent_Theobald" title="Frederick Vincent Theobald">Frederick Vincent Theobald</a> published his 5-volume monograph on the Culicidae.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Theobald1901&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theobald1901-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He had been provided with mosquito specimens sent in to the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum_(Natural_History)" class="mw-redirect" title="British Museum (Natural History)">British Museum (Natural History)</a> from around the world, on the 1898 instruction of the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">Secretary of State for the Colonies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a>, who had written that "in view of the possible connection of Malaria with mosquitoes, it is desirable to obtain exact knowledge of the different species of mosquitoes and allied insects in the various tropical colonies. I will therefore ask you ... to have collections made of the winged insects in the Colony which bite men or animals."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Harbach&#95;Kitching&#95;2016&#95;102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harbach_Kitching_2016-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</h3></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="External">External</h4></div>
<p>Mosquitoes are members of a <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">true flies (order Diptera)</a>: the Culicidae (from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">culex</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">culicis</i></span>, meaning "midge" or "gnat").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-isbn0-398-06179-3&#95;103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-398-06179-3-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are members of the infraorder <a href="/wiki/Culicomorpha" title="Culicomorpha">Culicomorpha</a> and superfamily <a href="/wiki/Culicoidea" title="Culicoidea">Culicoidea</a>. The phylogenetic tree is based on the FLYTREE project.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Internal">Internal</h4></div>
<p>The two subfamilies of mosquitoes are <a href="/wiki/Anophelinae" class="mw-redirect" title="Anophelinae">Anophelinae</a>, containing three genera and approximately 430 species, and <a href="/wiki/Culicinae" title="Culicinae">Culicinae</a>, which contains 11 tribes, 108 genera and 3,046 species. Kyanne Reidenbach and colleagues analysed mosquito <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetics</a> in 2009, using both nuclear DNA and morphology of 26 species. They note that Anophelinae is confirmed to be rather basal, but that the deeper parts of the tree are not well resolved.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reidenbach&#95;Cook&#95;Bertone&#95;Harbach&#95;2009&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reidenbach_Cook_Bertone_Harbach_2009-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="clade"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1344960044" />
